"27157"	"they"	"argue"	" Redwood immediately conceded defeat, saying Major had won ``fair and square.'' Senior Conservative politicians said the result was a clear victory for Major. Under party rules, Major needed a simple majority of the 329 Conservative legislators, or 165 votes, and 15 percent or 50 votes more than his nearest rival to regain leadership of the U.K.'s ruling Conservative Party and continue as prime minister. On June 22, Major stunned politicians and investors alike by resigning from the leadership of the ruling Conservative Party and sought an open re-election as party leader. His aim was to end three years of party infighting over Europe, in particular whether Britain should persist in leaving open the option of joining a future single European currency. On June 26, John Redwood, an anti-European cabinet minister, resigned from the cabinet to challenge Major declaring immediately that he would never abolish the pound. Superficially, the issue in the leadership election was whether Britain should agree to abolish the pound and adopt a future single European currency. In fact ``euroskeptic'' Conservatives legislators see sterling as a symbol of nationhood whose abolition, they argue, would mean giving up British sovereignty for membership in a European federal superstate dominated by Germany. Redwood's challenge as champion of the ``euroskeptic'' right was just as much a surprise as Major's resignation. The prime minister's principal challengers inside the cabinet have long been identified as Michael Heseltine, the pro-European Trade and Industry Secretary and Michael Portillo, the anti-European Employment Secretary. In the event, neither man so far come forward to challenge Major. Not only did Redwood put Major on the defensive over European policy, he also promised a 5 billion-pound tax cut immediately upon being elected. Major was then forced to concede future tax cuts were probably sending U.K. stock and bond prices lower and driving sterling down on concern that Britain's budget deficit would widen. </P> "	"nyt_eng_199507.gz"

"27238"	"they"	"argue"	" U.K. Prime Minister John Major named a new cabinet to shore up his Conservative Party's ailing fortunes one day after he won re-election as party leader. Facing an opposition Labour Party riding high in the polls, Major named Michael Heseltine, once a potential contender for his job and a renowned election campaigner, to the new post of deputy prime minister. Major, who must call an election by April 1997 at the latest, also appointed Heseltine as chairman of the important non-Treasury policymaking cabinet committees. With the appointments, Heseltine, a supporter of greater European economic unity who was formerly Trade and Industry Secretary, becomes the virtual public persona for the policies of the government and the Conservative Party. ``This is an important and vital position in running the government and in the presentation of the party,'' said a government official in Major's 10 Downing Street residence. ``This is a new and unique position.'' The prime minister also appointed Malcolm Rifkind, former Defense Secretary, as Foreign Secretary, to replace Douglas Hurd, who announced two weeks ago announced he was retiring from politics. Hurd was an ardent pro-European whereas Rifkind has adopted a more cautious approach to European policy in recent months. Analysts said his appointment may accordingly signal a less hospitable U.K. approach to closer European integration before next year's European inter-governmental conference on union. Major's new cabinet predictably drew a negative reaction from the Labour Party. John Prescott, Labour Deputy Leader, described the new line-up as ``pretty second rate.'' He added: ``It's all about being safe, it won't fizz with new ideas.'' The cabinet shakeup came the day after Major won a leadership election he called two weeks ago to end three years of party infighting over European integration. Major won 66 percent of the votes of the 329 Conservative legislators eligible to vote. His challenger, anti-European John Redwood, won 89 votes and there were eight abstentions, 12 spoiled ballots and two non-appearances. Financial markets reacted positively to the resolution of the uncertainty over Major's party leadership, though many analysts said the new cabinet won't improve the Conservatives' long-term prospects. ``I'm not sure the landscape has changed that much,'' said Nigel Richardson, head of bond research at Yamaichi International (Europe). ``The risk of having a Labour government sometime in the near future is still there.'' Major left Kenneth Clarke in the key job of Chancellor of the Exchequer. He also appointed William Waldegrave, a member of the party's center-left wing, to the job of Chief Secretary to the Treasury in charge of public spending. In another move designed to shore up voter support, Major merged the departments of Education and Employment, in line with his longstanding belief that the jobs of the future depend on higher educational standards. Gillian Shephard, former Education Secretary, will head the new department. In a sop to the party's anti-European wing, Michael Portillo was appointed Defense Secretary, a senior cabinet post, though overall Major did little to placate the party's anti-European rightwing, many of whom voted for Redwood. ``This is an obvious attempt to balance the right and the left but this would be viewed as a bit of a shift towards the left,'' said Andrew Milligan, chief economist at New Japan Securities. ``The right doesn't get a lot in this reshuffle.'' Major called the leadership election on June 22 to clear the air and win a ``vote of confidence'' from his parliamentary party after three years of party infighting principally over the European integration issue. Major's position on policies towards European economic and monetary union has been under attack from the anti-European right-wing of the Conservative Party since the British pound was forced out of the European exchange rate mechanism in September 1992. Major has refused to rule out future British membership in a future single European currency and that the question might have to be put to a referendum. His gambit was designed to end party infighting over Europe, in particular over whether Britain should persist in leaving open the option of joining a future single European }currency. Superficially, the issue in the leadership election was whether Britain should agree to abolish the pound and adopt a future single European currency. In fact, ``Euro-skeptic'' Conservatives legislators see sterling as a symbol of nationhood whose abolition, they argue, would mean giving up British sovereignty for membership in a European federal superstate dominated by Germany. However, there is little current indication that international investors, who have largely fought shy of U.K. financial markets in the past six months, will be encouraged. ``This will all be forgotten in two weeks,'' New Japan Securities' Milligan said. ``International investors haven't been assuaged.'' </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950705.0217 type=advis > <DATELINE>  (BC-FINANCIAL-NYT-BUDGET) </DATELINE> <TEXT> FOR THURSDAY AMs  Financial stories in sight at this hour from The New York Times News Service for newspapers of THURSDAY, July 6. INTERNATIONAL  COX-RUSSIA-AMERICANS _ MOSCOW _ Cowboy boots on the walls, California burgers, pina coladas. Americans, Europeans and Russians mixing at the bar. Welcome to Moscow 1995 and the American Bar &AMP; Grill _ one of dozens of new western oases that cater to a burgeoning expatriate community and growing Russian wealth. The cafes and shops mirror broadened opportunities that have transfigured lifestyles and made Moscow unrecognizable from its stodgy image of 10, even five, years ago. ``As a westerner, I have access to literally every type of food or commodity that I want _ one, if I have the time to find it, and two, if I'm willing to pay for it,'' said Steven Traylor, a 48-year-old construction engineer, sipping a draft at the bar. Once grudgingly open only to foreign diplomats, journalists or scholars, Russia today is a new frontier where everyone, from evangelists to investors, accountants to eco-activists, is trying to make his mark. There may be as many as 30,000 Americans living here today. A look at their lives. (Kunstel/Albright) With photos. To move Tuesday.  COX-SHEVARDNADZE _ TBILISI, Georgia _ The streets in this violent Caucasus republic are safer to walk. The economy is making a feeble start at recovery. The Russian bear has had his paw slapped. The beginnings of statehood here are the doing largely of Eduard Shevardnadze, the leader who even his friends worry has resorted to autocratic means to accomplish democratic ends. ``I think sometimes he plays not according to the rules. But do the the people against him play by the rules?'' asked Alexander Rondeli, a Shevardnadze advisor and professor of international relations. After detaining much of his political opposition in jail _ following an attempt on his life _ Shevardnadze feels secure enough to leave the country in the weeks leading up to Nov. 5 elections to address the United Nations, meet with President Clinton in New York on Tuesday and to huddle with congressional and administration officials in Washington. (Kunstel/Albright) To move Thursday.  Eds: Photo possible.  COX-MIDEAST-ECONOMY _ JERUSALEM _ The promise of profits has helped fuel the Middle East peace process. But economic gains have been stymied by the political bottlenecks in the emerging peace between Israelis, Palestinians and Jordanians. Political and business leaders from Israel and the Arab world will gather in Amman, Jordan, on Oct. 28 for a four-day conference, only the second of its kind to map a strategy of economic cooperation. The main goal of the Israelis is to convince Arabs that its already hearty economy won't dwarf its neighbors once trade is opened. (Holmes) To move Wednesday.  COX-POCAHONTAS _ GRAVESEND, England _ Add a legendary American Indian princess to an English town with a death-knell name and the result is one of the most improbable, and forgotten, fragments in Anglo-American history. Pocahontas _ the 17th century Indian princess, not the new Walt Disney film _ is buried on the grounds of St. George's Church in Gravesend, a Thames River town about 20 miles southeast of London. Her remains are thousands of miles from her tribal birthplace in what became Virginia. (Salome) To move Tuesday. With photos.  DUPLICATION ALERT: Moving in (i) international and (e) entertainment categories.  WASHINGTON  COX-POWELL-ADVISERS _ WASHINGTON _ Colin Powell has lots of friends, but does he have any advisers? It's hard to know who the former Joint Chiefs chairman is turning to - if anyone - for advice as he ponders whether to seek the presidency. This story will look at who _ other than his wife and kids _ is whispering in Powell's ear. (Shepard). Reskedded from last week.  COX-CPI _ WASHINGTON _ On the plus side, fiddling with the Consumer Price Index could save the federal government $281 billion over the next seven years. On the minus side, that would cost the average Social Security recipient more than $2,800. Veterans, disabled people and retired military and civil servants whose pensions and benefits are tied to the CPI would suffer similar losses. Taxpayers whose personal exemptions and tax brackets are tied to the CPI would also pay a little more in taxes. But some leaders in Congress believe that such changes would be less painful than the other cuts Congress contemplates as it tries to balance the federal budget within the next seven years. Besides, they argue, for years those recipients and taxpayers have been getting more than they should have. Their reasoning: Because the CPI measures price changes, not the cost of living, it goes up faster than the cost of living _ which should be the real basis for annual adjustments in benefits and tax rates. (Mollison)  COX-CPI-DATA _ How much the federal government would save and how much the average Social Security recipient would lose if Congress accepts an advisory ucommission's recommendation to base future cost-of-living adjustments on the CPI increase, minus one percentage point. WELLS _ Wells Fargo &AMP; Co. made an unsolicited offer to buy First Interstate Bancorp for about $10.8 billion in stock, seeking to form a banking powerhouse covering much of the western U.S. The acquisition would be one of the biggest U.S. bank mergers, second only to the planned $11.4 billion combination of Chemical Banking Corp. and Chase Manhattan Corp. announced in August. SOMEBODY GET HIM A HOBBY! Hal Davis, the most dangerous editor at National Law Journal (hdavisljextra.com) since he got his Internet log-on, found and forwarded this:  ``Wouldn't the sentence `I want to put a hyphen between the words Fish and And and And and Chips in my Fish-And-Chips sign' have been clearer if quotation marks had been placed before Fish, and between Fish and and, and and and And, and And and and, and and and And, and And and and, and and and Chips, as well as after Chips?''  Address replies to Hal directly.  ALL RIGHT, WE'LL DO IT, BUT WE YANKS ARE STILL GOING TO MISS THOSE KICKY LITTLE U'S IN `NEIGHBOUR.' A dialogue uncovered in the Usenet news group alt.english.usage:  Elliot (mppe4central.susx.ac.uk): ``English is spoke in England. That's why it's called English. The term `British English' is a pathetic nonsense.''  Richard Kaulfuss (dkaulfboehme.demon.co.uk): ``Though not very well, apparently. In fact you're wrong. If anything, the converse is more likely to be true. If you took your head out of the sand for a little while, you would realise that English is no longer a single language (not that it ever was), but a family, and British/Australian/Indian/Black/Scots English are all perfectly valid descriptions of members of that family. Like it or not, American English will be the undisputed world standard within the next 50 years, if it isn't already, and all other varieties will be described with reference to it.''  Rex Knepp (kneppstarbase.neosoft.com): ``Shouldn't you be speaking United-Kingdomish then, Elliot?''  Peter Drew (petedrewinnotts.co.uk) of Franchlodin Petkats, Nottingham (!): ``I always speak in United Kingdom Of Great British And Northern Irish. I think you'll find that infinitely more intelligible than United Kingdom Of Not-So-Good British And Southern Irish.''  IN FLUSHING, N.Y., NEARLY EVERYBODY READS The Flushing Tribune.  FOUND IN THE CARBONS BIN. Here is an excerpt from News From the Net, the Chief Copyboy's other column on the New York Times News Service, the most noble wire service of them all. This column should appear in every English-language newspaper in the world before all our most literate readers abandon us entirely in favor of something cybernetically satisfying:  ``It's just like real life. On the Internet, women are a recurring subject of conversation, if not a standing reason for a bar brawl.  ``This bugs women.  ``They don't like being hit on, put down or held up to ridicule on the `Net. When they post a comment in any of the thousands of Usenet newsgroups, they hate to read condescending drivel from some male geek, or get sexual propositions from a dozen of them. Women don't like men who log on with female- sounding names to tease other men, or to try to worm into women's on-line discussions.  ``It keeps many women from logging on at all. How many? Who knows? But most estimates put the male-to-female on-line ratio at about 7-to-3, 6-to-4, around there.  ``So women probably will welcome Judith Broadhurst's new book, `The Woman's Guide to Online Services' (McGraw-Hill, $19.95), a primer for computer users who reason without the X chromosome. It covers all the standard how-tos, with tips on hardware and on-line services, and many special forums and services for women.  ``Stacy Horn, founder of a woman's on-line service called Echo in New York (information via e-mail at echo(at)echonyc.com), says it thus:  `` `If you're not wired or in some way reachable by e-mail, you're going to fall out of so many information loops that are available online. You used to have to go to luncheons and cocktail parties to network. Now you have to be online.' ''  And: (Subhed) Calling the law  ``Keyboard cruisers don't have to give up their legalese, even if they miss Murder One over on the house's conventional screen. Misc.legal is a group for posting questions of law, answering same, and arguing the herein aforementioned. And many of the contributors are lawyers or know what they're talking about.  ``Someone borrowed your car, dinged something, fled the scene, and now it's back in your driveway and there's a gas- masked officer talking to you from behind the ring of cruisers through a bullhorn? Probably too late for Usenet to be useful, but you should see how steamed the (group's other) contributors are, arguing about that multimillion-dollar McDonald's hot-coffee verdict.''  Thus endeth the excerpt. Editors don't need the gift of prophecy to see the advantage of regularly reading thousands of discussion forums, advice columns, political debates and bulletin boards. Our former readers certainly are going to Usenet. If you're not already a client of NYTNS, contact author Charley Stough at addresses below for special arrangements.  OOPS, HOLIDAY GIFT TIME AGAIN. Where do those calendar pages go? Well, there's still plenty of time to get examples of those BONG Collection typewriter-jewelry goodies. When you give presents to people, they owe you presents back, see, and the return volley will certainly be worth more than these:  Earrings or tie tacks/lapel pins, $12.50. The BONG Pejoratives Poster, listing more than 120 newspapers' nasty names, $3. Genuine white oak deskplates (17 letters max.), $39.50; with two lines of wording, $49.50. All except the poster are made from typewriter keys in various colors and faces, and delivered postpaid to North American addresses. And oh yeah, the Chagrin Falls Commercial Scimitar Foreign Correspondent press card, buck and a half.  Order from Charley Stough at BONG Center, 2517 Rugby Road, Dayton, Ohio 45406.  COMIX SECTION. The Further Adventures of Herman ``Speed'' Graphic, ace photographer for the Chagrin Falls Commercial Scimitar, and his Faithful Companion, Typo the Wonder Pig.  PANEL ONE: Doffing his trenchcoat, a deathbed gift from an ancient mystic wire service executive editor on a fog-shrouded eastern island, Speed takes his place next to Typo in front of the Photo Lab computer terminal. Typo greets him warmly, ``Great staff-meeting outburst, Boss! I'm already pulling in the scoring!''  PANEL TWO: Typo reports, ``OK, Layout Desk. The comment is Colorful display 3.7, vocabulary 4.3, startle quotient 4.9! Good score, Boss!' ''  Speed remarks, ``Well, I gave it all I had, but they're easy! What about the News Desk?''  PANEL THREE: Typo declares, ``Here they are now, Boss! Inspired use of prop, drop-kicking lectern over meeting table, 4.89 points! Cocoa spillage by visiting graphics consultant, a full Five for Secondary Effect! This is historic, Boss!''  Speed observes modestly, ``Yeah, but News desk guys are so impressionable! Why, at staff meetings back during the Streaking Craze ... !''  PANEL FOUR: Typo gasps, ``The big one, Boss! Features Desk, inventors of the Career-Enhancing Tantrum, announce ... Piercing Shriek, 5.0! Shredding Tearsheets, 5.0! Chandelier as Trapeze, 5.0! ... And finally, Technical Points ...! What, Boss? Only a 2?!''  INTERPANEL BOX: Speed growls, ``The fix is in.''  PANEL FIVE: Sitting safely on the ledge outside the 8th-floor photo lab window, Speed reviews the critique sheet, grumbling, ``Technical! Just because I was trying to get a 7-column photo on a 6-column page! It's the old built-in prejudice against picture people! Those word guys get all the breaks!''  Typo remonstrates, ``Aw, Boss, think of the good side! Features Editor Hyperba Lee can't stay hysterical forever, and a good tantrum raises morale in the whole newsroom! And besides, to replace the movie reviewer you gotta win best-of-five tantrums anyway!''  A connoisseur of great insubordinations, BONG Chief Copyboy Charley Stough, Dayton Daily News, 45 S. Ludlow St., Dayton, Ohio 45401 salutes NYTNS growlers worldwide! Phone (513) 225- 2445 after 3 p.m. eastern. Fax 225-2489. INTERNATIONAL  COX-RUSSIA-US _ The Russians are in a sour mood toward the West, and that sentiment hangs like a dark cloud over Monday's meeting in Hyde Park, N.Y., between President Boris Yeltsin and President Clinton. Analysis. (Kunstel/Albright 700).  EDs: MOVED Wednesday.  COX-RUSSIA-AMERICANS _ MOSCOW _ Cowboy boots on the walls, California burgers, pina coladas. Americans, Europeans and Russians mixing at the bar. Welcome to Moscow 1995 and the American Bar &AMP; Grill _ one of dozens of new western oases that cater to a burgeoning expatriate community and growing Russian wealth. The cafes and shops mirror broadened opportunities that have transfigured lifestyles and made Moscow unrecognizable from its stodgy image of 10, even five, years ago. ``As a westerner, I have access to literally every type of food or commodity that I want _ one, if I have the time to find it, and two, if I'm willing to pay for it,'' said Steven Traylor, a 48-year-old construction engineer, sipping a draft at the bar. Once grudgingly open only to foreign diplomats, journalists or scholars, Russia today is a new frontier where everyone, from evangelists to investors, accountants to eco-activists, is trying to make his mark. There may be as many as 30,000 Americans living here today. A look at their lives. (Kunstel/Albright 750&AMP;add).  EDs: Story MOVED Tuesday. Photos, COX1-COX2, MOVED Tuesday.  COX-SHEVARDNADZE _ TBILISI, Georgia _ The streets in this violent Caucasus republic are safer to walk. The economy is making a feeble start at recovery. The Russian bear has had his paw slapped. The beginnings of statehood here are the doing largely of Eduard Shevardnadze, the leader who even his friends worry has resorted to autocratic means to accomplish democratic ends. ``I think sometimes he plays not according to the rules. But do the the people against him play by the rules?'' asked Alexander Rondeli, a Shevardnadze advisor and professor of international relations. After detaining much of his political opposition in jail _ following an attempt on his life _ Shevardnadze feels secure enough to leave the country in the weeks leading up to Nov. 5 elections to address the United Nations, meet with President Clinton in New York on Tuesday and to huddle with congressional and administration officials in Washington. (Kunstel/Albright).  EDs: To move Thursday. Photos will be available.  COX-MIDEAST-ECONOMY _ JERUSALEM _ The promise of profits has helped fuel the Middle East peace process. But economic gains have been stymied by the political bottlenecks in the emerging peace between Israelis, Palestinians and Jordanians. Political and business leaders from Israel and the Arab world will gather in Amman, Jordan, on Oct. 28 for a four-day conference, only the second of its kind to map a strategy of economic cooperation. The main goal of the Israelis is to convince Arabs that its already hearty economy won't dwarf its neighbors once trade is opened. (Holmes) To move Thursday.  COX-MIDEAST-BOX.  EDs: MOVED Wednesday.  COX-POCAHONTAS _ GRAVESEND, England _ Add a legendary American Indian princess to an English town with a death-knell name and the result is one of the most improbable, and forgotten, fragments in Anglo-American history. Pocahontas _ the 17th century Indian princess, not the new Walt Disney film _ is buried on the grounds of St. George's Church in Gravesend, a Thames River town about 20 miles southeast of London. Her remains are thousands of miles from her tribal birthplace in what became Virginia. (Salome 740).  DUPLICATION ALERT: Moved in (i) international and (e) entertainment categories.  EDs: MOVED Tuesday. Photos, COX9-COX11, MOVED overnight Wednesday.  WASHINGTON  COX-POWELL-ADVISERS _ WASHINGTON _ Colin Powell has lots of friends, but does he have any advisers? It's hard to know who the former Joint Chiefs chairman is turning to - if anyone - for advice as he ponders whether to seek the presidency. This story will look at who _ other than his wife and kids _ is whispering in Powell's ear. (Shepard).  EDs: To move Friday afternoon. Reskedded from last week.  COX-CPI _ WASHINGTON _ On the plus side, fiddling with the Consumer Price Index could save the federal government $281 billion over the next seven years. On the minus side, that would cost the average Social Security recipient more than $2,800. Veterans, disabled people and retired military and civil servants whose pensions and benefits are tied to the CPI would suffer similar losses. Taxpayers whose personal exemptions and tax brackets are tied to the CPI would also pay a little more in taxes. But some leaders in Congress believe that such changes would be less painful than the other cuts Congress contemplates as it tries to balance the federal budget within the next seven years. Besides, they argue, for years those recipients and taxpayers have been getting more than they should have. Their reasoning: Because the CPI measures price changes, not the cost of living, it goes up faster than the cost of living _ which should be the real basis for annual adjustments in benefits and tax rates. (Mollison)  COX-CPI-DATA _ How much the federal government would save and how much the average Social Security recipient would lose if Congress accepts an advisory ucommission's recommendation to base future cost-of-living adjustments on the CPI increase, minus one percentage point. Times News Service  Albert Speer: His Battle With Truth By Gitta Sereny. Vintage, $18. The author writes that Speer was the lone man in Hitler's inner circle to wrestle with his conscience. But she also demonstrates that Speer was dishonest with others and probably himself over just how much he knew. ``The effect is stunning, a veritable `Rashomon' of Nazi genocide,'' Claudia Koonz wrote here in 1995.  Atticus By Ron Hansen. Harper Perennial, $12. In a kind of contemporary retelling of the parable of the prodigal son, Atticus, an aging former rancher, enjoys a Christmas visit from his younger child, Scott, a dissolute painter. Soon after, he is informed of Scott's suicide in Mexico. Although Atticus' own investigation reveals that the death was a murder, this novel is less a mystery than an exploration of motive and identity. ``Mr. Hansen writes vigorously, and like an angel,'' our reviewer, Janet Burroway, said last year. The same publisher has also reissued two of the author's earlier works.  The Assassination Of Jesse James By The Coward Robert Ford ($12) depicts the moral ambivalence of James and the peculiar relationship he had with Ford, a sycophant who ultimately killed him. The evocation of their eerie partnership is ``vivid and sustained,'' David Freeman wrote in these pages in 1984. The Old West is also the setting for Desperadoes ($12), which centers on the exploits of the Dalton gang, peace officers turned murderous rustlers. The novel opens in 1937 as the Daltons' last survivor mulls over their past, which Hollywood is about to re-create. ``The writing is so accomplished and the book has such an authoritative tone that one finds it difficult to think of this as a first novel,'' Jerome Charyn said in the Book Review in 1979.  Lewis Carroll: A Biography By Morton N. Cohen. Vintage, $17. The author of so much immortal nonsense was also a brilliant mathematician and a religious man who condemned himself for sinning. But Cohen believes that whatever Carroll's feelings were, his behavior toward children was innocent. This book is ``entertaining and convincing,'' Peter Ackroyd wrote here in 1995. Carroll's talent for satire is itself parodied in Dreamhouse, by Alison Habens (Picador USA, $14). In this wry updating of ``Alice's Adventures in Wonderland,'' a heroine named Celia eats drug-tainted tarts and has an identity crisis. Last year our reviewer, Lisa Zeidner, said, ``There's a genuine pleasure to be had in thrusting cross-dressers, hip-hop and the feminist rhetoric of date rape into last century's story.''  Yugoslavia: Death of a Nation By Laura Silber and Allan Little. Penguin, $12.95. Two British journalists use detailed interviews and documentation to show that the Serbian president, Slobodan Milosevic, deliberately started the bloody Balkan conflict. Europe also bears responsibility, they argue, for prematurely recognizing Croatia. Their book ``is one of the finest volumes to come out of the war,'' Chris Hedges wrote in these pages in 1996.  The moral aspects of the devastation are the focus of Love Thy Neighbor: A Story of War, by Peter Maass (Vintage, $13). The author condemns the West for not aiding the victims of ethnic cleansing; at the same time, he emphasizes that the Serbs' behavior exemplified capacities of which we cannot conveniently absolve ourselves. He ``sees all this, feels it deeply and conveys it beautifully,'' Brian Hall said in the Book Review last year.  Blackwater By Kerstin Ekman. Translated by Joan Tate. Picador USA, $15. This novel revolves around a murder in the Scandinavian wilderness, but, like ``Smilla's Sense of Snow,'' it deals with more: the destruction of the environment, the wounds of history and the complexities of the heart. It is ``a rich adventure, the kind of long, lush, thoughtful page-turner many of us crave but rarely get our hands on,'' Beverly Lowry wrote here last year.  The Myth Of Wild Africa: Conservation Without Illusion By Jonathan S. Adams and Thomas O. McShane. University of California, $14.95. The authors' thesis is that endangered animals cannot be saved unless the needs of the people who live among them are also considered. With more incentives, they write, local Africans might help more species. ``The authors . . . make the complexity of the continent, its ecosystems and its political problems abundantly clear,'' our reviewer, Robert S. O. Harding, said in 1992.  The difficulties of conservation also emerge in Of Tigers and Men: Entering the Age of Extinction, by Richard Ives (Avon, $12.50). Written by a naturalist who journeyed through Asia to see a tiger in the wild, the book is an elegy for a vanishing world. It ``is a poignant inquiry into the place of man and nature,'' Raymond Bonner wrote in the Book Review last year.  Toward Amnesia By Sarah Van Arsdale. Riverhead, $11. A marine biologist reacts to the end of her long affair with another woman by pursuing oblivion _ even to the point of moving to a faraway island and assuming a new identity. ``The novel comes to life when Ms. Van Arsdale turns her observant eye to the narrator's clumsy bumps into the real world,'' Susannah Hunnewell said in these pages in 1996. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19970303.0394 type=story > <HEADLINE> COUPLE MAY WANT TO MODERATE STOCK POSITIONS </HEADLINE> <DATELINE>  (BC-FINANCE-HOOKER-BOS) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199703.gz"

NATIONAL NEWS (``a'' category)  PELICAN-REVIVAL (Point Lobos State Reserve, Calif.) _ The endangered California brown pelican hasn't nested along the shoreline of Carmel Bay for more than 30 years. Yet things may be changing. Hundreds of brown pelicans are swarming around the point and its little offshore rock islands, raising hope that the remarkable seabird knocked back by the pesticide DDT will once again return to mate, build nests and hatch young in Central California. By JANE KAY.  BUSINESS/FINANCIAL NEWS (``f'' category)  COMPUTER-COLUMN (Undated) _ According to some technopundits, the future of computing is in the palm of your hand. Hand-held computing devices, they argue, will soon supplant the PC as the primary way people communicate and access the Internet. I've long been a skeptic of this point of view. Hand-helds are cool for retrieving information, but frustrating for storing and massaging it. By DWIGHT SILVERMAN.  NEW-AIRPLANE (San Antonio) _ Sino Swearingen Aircraft Co. has unveiled the first completed model of its SJ30-2 jet, taking a major step toward becoming the first start-up company to bring a business jet to market in 40 years. If certified by the Federal Aviation Administration, the San Antonio company's six-passenger, $5 million plane will be the first business jet sold by a start-up since the Learjet was introduced in the early 1960s. By SANFORD NOWLIN.  LAWYERS-MERGE (San Francisco) _ Old-line San Francisco law giant Pillsbury Madison &AMP; Sutro is girding its 126-year-old practice for the new economy by agreeing to join forces with the old-line New York-based law firm of Winthrop Stimson Putnam &AMP; Roberts, both firms announced. By DEMIAN BULWA.  WEB-FINANCES (San Francisco) _ Now that banks, credit card providers, brokerages and other financial companies have succeeded in getting customers to move their business online, the money business is betting the next most popular service will be Web sites that allow customers to consolidate all their financial transactions in one place. By CHRISTIAN BERTHELSEN.  CAREER-COLUMN (San Francisco) _ I love asking job applicants, ``What's your favorite Web site?'' It's mostly a throwaway question, but what I like about it is that the best answers are honest ones, not rehearsed ones. What am I looking for? A pilot light, some sign that the person in front of me is enthused about something on Earth other than the particular job I'm offering. By DAVE MURPHY.  ENTERTAINMENT(``e'' category)  TV-NEW-SHOWS (Pasadena, Calif.) _ After the networks spent a year freaking out _ first over the surprise success of ``Who Wants to Be Millionaire,'' then the ``Survivor'' reality phenomenon _ they neglected to take care of one teeny, bottom-of-the-pile task: developing a really good show for next year. By MARK McGUIRE.  KID-BAND (Lake George, N.Y.) _ Dylan and Chelsea Wilcox, 12 and 14 respectively, went to a park to play _ in a different sense of the word. The siblings, who live in White Creek, N.Y., are the principal members of the band GreayStar, which recently signed a seven-album deal with Epic Records. By MIKE HESS.  LIFESTYLE/FEATURES (``l'' category)  DICTIONARY-SLANG (Undated) _ Slang is increasingly becoming part of common language. In the 2000 edition of the Random House Webster's College Dictionary, there 303 new words appearing for the first time among the 200,000-plus definitions. The majority of the new words come from slang: my bad! (my mistake) 24/7 (continually, constantly) and candy (someone or something that is excellent, pleasing or pleasurable). By KRISTEN MACK.  FLORAL-FROGS (Albany, N.Y.) _ In addition to being functional, floral frogs have also become very collectible. Some people collect artistic figural frogs, which are shaped like animals or have a statue rising out of the center. Bathing beauty flower frogs (of women in swimsuits) were made in Germany in the 1920s. English Glass frogs produced in the 1920s and '30s were Britain's answer to American Depression Glass. By FRANCES INGRAHAM HEINS.  TRAVEL (``t'' category)  PROVINCETOWN (Provincetown, Mass.) _ The summer season is now at full bore in Provincetown and will not slow down until mid-September. At the northern tip of the Outer Cape, this town is renowned for its art galleries, cafes, nightclubs, clever landscaped alleys, colorful cottages and guests houses and family-friendly/gay-friendly attitude. By WILLIAM M. DOWD.  SPORTS (``s'' category)  OLY-WOMEN-DISCUS (Sacramento, Calif.) _ There is an elegance to the women's discus throw that often gets lost in the swirl of activity in most track meets. At the U.S. Olympic Track &AMP; Field Trials, UCLA's Seilala Sua threw a near American record throw of 216 feet, 2 inches. By JOHN CRUMPACKER.  FBN-NINERS (Stockton, Calif.) _ When John Milem was a Marine recruit, his drill instructor at Parris Island (S.C.) would make the young man get on his knees. Then the sergeant would stand on Milem's footlocker. All this so the DI could look down on him. Milem is a 6-foot-9, 290-pound defensive end in 49ers training camp, a tough former barroom bouncer who looks like your average Marine _ only bigger and stronger. By BRUCE ADAMS.  POLO-CLUB (Saratoga Springs, N.Y.) _ The new owner of Saratoga Polo Club is investing $1.5 million in improvements and charging significantly more for memberships to raise Saratoga polo to a world-class level. ``We're hoping to attract the best teams in the country, as well as from abroad,'' said polo pioneer William Ylvisaker, former chairman of the U.S. Polo Association. By DENNIS YUSKO.  COMMENTARY (``k'' category)  MORSE-COLUMN (San Francisco) _ If you're of a certain age, you probably remember your first flight on an airliner as fondly as I do. SALTER-COLUMN (San Francisco) _ The subject has replaced all others in my circle of friends and acquaintances. TOBACCO-DEATH (San Francisco) _ On Friday, a Miami jury gave its verdict in a class action suit against the tobacco industry, the first to go to trial: a $145 billion judgment for the plaintiffs. COMMENTARY  A NATION OF ERIN BROCKOVICH WANNABES: CORPORATE LIABILITY IN USA  LAWSUIT-CRAZY-USA-ECONOMIST  Most Americans say they hate lawyers. In America, even some lawyers say they hate lawyers. But everybody prefers lawyers to evil blood-sucking corporations. This love-hate relationship is a national obsession, but using the law to bash business can get out of hand. At its best, America's corporate-liability regime encourages companies to serve the public better. At its worst, though, it fails to spur good conduct and merely transfers wealth from taxpayers and consumers to lawyers, and often resembles legalized extortion.  (ATTN: Commentary Editors)  (800 words)  NATO INFIGHTING: HEY, THE ENEMY'S OUT THERE, BOYS  NATO-IMPLODES-ECONOMIST  If there could ever be a good moment for Europeans and Americans to relish another Balkan imbroglio, the current fighting by ethnic-Albanians attempting to destabilize Macedonia is not it. Even if they squeeze safely past this new Balkan danger, will the EU's ambitions to carry more weight in the world, and George W. Bush's ambivalence about matters abroad, help Europe and America pull together, or pull apart?  (ATTN: Commentary Editors)  (950 words)  IS THE WORLD HEADING FOR ITS FIRST GLOBAL RECESSION?  GLOBAL-RECESSION?-ECONOMIST  Last year, the world economy enjoyed spectacular growth of almost 5 percent _ its fastest for 16 years. This year it looks much less buoyant. America and Japan, the two biggest economies, accounting for 46 percent of world output, are both teetering on the brink of recession. Stock markets almost everywhere are tumbling. Is the world heading into recession?  (ATTN: Commentary Editors)  (1,025 words)  U.N. MEMBERS SHOULD CENSURE CHINA. EU HYPOCRITES WON'T  SPINELESS-EU-CHINA-ECONOMIST  Those who prefer quiet diplomacy to the megaphone kind often argue that shouting at the Chinese government about the way it treats its people is counterproductive. Far better, they argue, to work with the Chinese, not against them. The trouble with this approach is that it does not seem to be working. So the Americans have this year, at last, tabled a motion of censure at the annual meeting of the U.N. Commission on Human Rights. And once again, Europe has chosen the spineless option.  (ATTN: Commentary Editors)  (700 words)  CAN CAVALLO LOOSEN ARGENTINA'S ECONOMIC STRAITJACKET?  DEBT-FRETS-ARGENTINA-ECONOMIST  It is fitting that Domingo Cavallo should have been drafted this week as Argentina's new economy minister _ the third person to hold that job this month. He is now seen as the only man capable of maintaining the currency-board system that he set up a decade ago. Not only has the device killed hyperinflation; for much of the 1990s, it delivered strong growth. But Argentina's economy has now been locked in a recession for almost three years; the currency board has become a straightjacket. So what can Cavallo do?  (ATTN: Commentary Editors)  (775 words)  INDIA'S SCANDAL PROVES INTERNET IS CHANGING POLITICS  CYBER-WHISTLEBLOWERS-ECONOMIST  Bangaru Laxman, the president of the main party in India's ruling coalition, made several mistakes. One was to accept some money that looked very much like a bribe. Another was to take it without checking the credentials of those who offered it. But Laxman also had bad luck. His temptation took place in the Internet age, when not all journalists are constrained by lumbering technology and nervous proprietors. Clearly, the Internet is opening up government everywhere to scrutiny on a scale that has never been seen before.  (ATTN: Commentary Editors)  (675 words)  USA  SEATTLE: BOEING, BOEING...GONE  BOEING-BYEBYE-SE-ECONOMIST  SEATTLE _ Despite the arrival of Microsoft, Amazon and Starbucks, Seattle is inextricably linked with one company: Boeing. With 80,000 employees in the area and one of the best-known names in the world (to Seattle, Coca-Cola is merely a giveaway refreshment on a 747), Boeing has given the city an economic engine and civic identity. So the announcement on March 21 by Boeing's boss that the company was moving its corporate headquarters out of Seattle was a shock far more devastating than the real earthquake that struck three weeks ago.  (ATTN: SEATTLE...and Commentary Editors)  (500 words)  FOOT-IN-MOUTH DISEASE STRIKES BUSH'S FINANCE TEAM  (UNDATED) _ In Washington, it is never too early to start claiming credit or apportioning blame. Under Bill Clinton, the Democrats said that they had built ``a new economy'' long before the numbers hinted that one might actually exist. Now the two political parties are blaming one another for a recession that hasn't arrived yet. The cynical ``who lost the economy?'' debate might be written off as just another political game. But it matters, not least because it raises early questions about President Bush and his economic team.  (ATTN: Commentary Editors)  (1,050 words)  `ROMANCE IN A BOTTLE' HAS SOUTHERN BELLES SWOONING  EROTIC-TONIC-ECONOMIST  LITTLE ROCK _ Something strange has been happening in Little Rock. Women from around the South have been descending on Arkansas's state capital and making their way to the Wycoff Coffee House. The reason is Niagara _ a blue fizzy Swedish tonic, for which the Wycoff's owner, Lari Williams, is the sole American distributor. It is not unusual for her to find a line of frenzied Southern belles lined up outside the glass door of the Wycoff to buy the herbal drink. Why? Because it is ``romance in a bottle'' _ a passion potion that reportedly possesses an erotic recipe to make women's _ and some men's _ libidos soar to new heights.  (ATTN: Little Rock, Ark....and Southern Editors)  (450 words)  USS GREENEVILLE RAISES QUESTIONS OF MILITARY ACCOUNTABILITY  GREENEVILLE-GUILT-ECONOMIST  WASHINGTON _ The United States may increasingly have to live with the prospect of non-American authorities becoming involved when its troops are accused of wrongdoing in far-flung parts of the world. But some sensitive test-cases could arise if the United States sticks by Bill Clinton's commitment to join an international criminal court. Under the proposed ICC rules, countries will be safe from prosecution by the court if, and only if, they are seen to be conducting their own investigation into alleged atrocities by their troops. But what if America's armed forces were ever accused of failing to investigate their own misdeeds in a convincing way? Should that happen, America might find itself hauled before the ICC for war crimes.  (ATTN: Commentary Editors)  (825 words)  EUROPE  FROM MILOSEVIC TO MONARCHY?  SERB-MONARCHY?-ECONOMIST  CACAK, SERBIA _ ``A modern monarchy for a modern Serbia.'' It is in those rather paradoxical terms that Velimir Ilic, the burly provincial mayor who masterminded last year's uprising against Slobodan Milosevic, imagines the future of his country. For the majority of Serbs struggling to scrape a living and fretting about the violence on their country's southern fringe, the restoration of the Karadjordjevic dynasty, deposed after the Second World War, is not the most urgent priority. But not so in the town of Cacak, whose mayor and citizens spearheaded the storming of the Belgrade parliament last October. If they have their way, Serbia will go from a Milosevic autocracy to a constitutional monarchy.  (400 words)  INTERNATIONAL  SINCE CIVIL WAR, SUDAN'S NUBA HAVE GONE BACK IN TIME  `STONE-AGE'-SUDAN-ECONOMIST  IUWIRI, SUDAN _ Since the civil war began 17 years ago, the Nuba Mountains in central Sudan have been going back in time. Even ``back to the Stone Age,'' says Daniel Kodi, a Nuba native and founding member of the rebel Sudanese People's Liberation Army (SPLA). When the Nuba threw their lot with the SPLA in 1985, the government imposed a stifling blockade. Despite the largest humanitarian airlift in history, little aid now gets to the Nuba. The government does not let food into areas it is trying to capture from the rebels _ and the Nuba Mountains is one such area.  (525 words)  ASIA  DOING BUSINESS IN DANANG  VIETNAM-BIZ-LIFE-ECONOMIST  DANANG _ If you go to Cong's pavement cafe in Danang, you may have to hang on tightly to your seat. The police, you see, come by almost every other day to enforce their ban against street cafes _ by taking whatever furniture they can lay their hands on. When Cong sees them coming, he simply throws all his plastic chairs and tables into the river, which runs alongside. Later he jumps in to retrieve them. ``The police are afraid to go in,'' he laughs. Such is business life in Vietnam. But as the state continues to reduce its role in the economy, people are gaining more latitude to do as they please, even in areas far removed from the hustle and bustle around Ho Chi Minh City.  (525 words)  BUSINESS  COMING SOON TO A SCREEN NEAR YOU: A REVAMPED PC OPERATING SYSTEM  REVAMPED-PC-SYSTEMS-ECONOMIST  (UNDATED) _ Coming soon to a screen near you: a revamp for your computer's operating system. Microsoft's new Windows XP, announced in February, will come as standard on almost all new PCs by the second half of the year. Yet although it has 92 percent of the market, Windows does not quite rule the desktop. On March 24, Apple launches the latest version of its operating system for Macintosh computers, Mac OS X (pronounced ``oh-ess-ten''), which it hopes will boost the Mac's market share above its current measly 4 percent. Both firms hope that their new operating systems will boost sales of new computers, and breathe some life into the ailing PC industry.  (800 words)  `I'M READY FOR MY PICKET LINE, MR. DEMILLE'  STRIKES-TINSELTOWN-ECONOMIST  LOS ANGELES _ Hollywood is in its most self-congratulatory mood at Oscar time. But after the awards have been handed out on March 25 and the champagne corks have stopped popping, Tinseltown has to roll up its sleeves quickly and get back to business. A series of strikes threatened by writers and actors could shut down most of the movie-making industry. It could be a long, bitter and crippling battle, the effects of which could last far longer than any strike. Indeed, if a strike did drag on, the only Oscar to be awarded in 2002 could be for ``Best Foreign Film.''  (TIMELY for Sunday's Academy Awards, March 25!) Times Week in Review for Sunday, June 3, moved Saturday.  (Editors: All clients receive all budgets, but only full-service clients receive all stories. Please check your level of service to determine which stories you will receive.)  On the dress page:  RACE-CENSUS-REVIEW (Washington) _ The apportionment of political power on the basis of population, the use of race in the drawing of legislative districts and racial bloc voting have prompted people to inflate their numbers. Or at least fear their dilution. That was why several civil rights groups and the Congressional Black Caucus opposed allowing people to choose more than one race on the 2000 census. But when the politics of racial numbers ran headlong into the desire of interracial couples to not have their children be forced to choose one race, the result was a Solomonic decision that may give a distorted picture of the nation's racial landscape. By Steven A. Holmes. (Moved in the `a' category)  RACE-PROFILING-REVIEW (Los Angeles) _ The growing national crusade against racial profiling is being called the civil rights movement of this generation. Polls have shown that a majority of Americans believe profiling is widespread, and an even greater majority disapprove of it. Yet throughout the country, the evidence for its existence is largely anecdotal. For that reason, the first wave of legislation and public policy initiatives designed to address the problem focus on the collection of data intended to substantiate allegations of racial bias in policing. By Gregory Rodriguez. (Moved in the `a' category)  BRITAIN-RACE-REVIEW (Oldham, England) _ Two things were striking about the aftermath of the race riots that raged through Oldham in the British Midlands last week in the midst of a national election campaign. First, none of the party leaders _ not Tony Blair, the Labor prime minister, nor William Hague, the Conservative leader, nor Charles Kennedy, leader of the Liberal Democrats _ found it convenient to visit Oldham and speak to the people there. Second, nobody even seemed to notice. By Sarah Lyall. (Moved in the `i' category)  _ Also moved in the `a' category:  GUNMAKERS-DIVERSIFY-REVIEW (Undated) _ Political battles over gun ownership may rage, but there's a consensus that guns are not a growth industry. Production for domestic and overseas handgun sales declined 52 percent between 1993 and 1999. The number of licensed gun dealers has fallen to 104,000 from a high of 284,000 in 1992. Twenty years ago, 1 in 14 Americans was a licensed hunter. Today, it's 1 in 18. Gun companies are, above all else, companies, and the buzzword of the moment is diversification. Smith &AMP; Wesson, for instance, has opened retail stores where customers can buy the gun maker's logo on jewelry, gym bags, mountain bikes _ even teddy bears. By Simon Romero.  BUGS-BUNNY-BIAS-REVIEW (Undated) _ After Cartoon Network acquired the rights to all Bugs Bunny cartoons, it announced it would hold a marathon 49-hour broadcast of the entire Bugs oeuvre _ the first complete airing ever. Then the network blinked. Twelve cartoons would not be aired because they contained racially offensive material, the network said. The decision raises questions about popular culture and about how media corporations like AOL Time Warner and Disney, which control the historical record of cartoons, will preserve or erase objectionable chapters of their past. By John Leland.  SHAME-PENALTIES-REVIEW (Undated) _ Modern America may seem far removed from Puritan New England, but the idea that punishment should be based on public humiliation has never disappeared. If anything, it seems to be resurgent, especially for offenses deemed to involve morality, such as sex crimes, drunk driving, prostitution and wife beating. Bringing morality into the courts in this way could have far-reaching implications for how America administers and regards justice. Some of the most serious concerns center on the separation of church and state and whether shaming is actually religion-in-the-courtroom in disguise. By Dean E. Murphy.  POWER-PRICE-CAPS-REVIEW (Undated) _ President Bush says price controls on electricity would do nothing to increase supply, but 10 prominent economists have sent a letter to the president calling for the federal government to clamp down on wholesale energy prices. Nostrums suitable to the textbook world of perfect competition, they argue, do not fit the messy facts of electricity markets. It might seem like a man-bites-dog story, but the idea of economists calling for price controls isn't nearly as zany as it appears. Electricity prices skyrocketed in California in part for standard supply-and-demand reasons. By Michael Weinstein.  SCOTUS-DISABILITY-REVIEW (Washington) _ The Supreme Court's ruling in PGA v. Martin defined the issue as one principally about the nature of golf _ and not about what constitutes a disability, and was therefore not a difficult case, as the 7-2 margin indicates. The hard decisions lie ahead. Eleven years after passage of what was arguably the most important civil rights law of the past quarter-century, the essential concepts of the Americans With Disabilities Act remain undefined. In many cases brought under the act, the question of whether there is a disability _ and whether the act therefore applies at all _ is in dispute. By Linda Greenhouse.  NY-SOCIETY-REVIEW (New York) _ Fluidity has always defined New York society, ever since the Dutch settlers opened their gates to the more-moneyed English. New York was always more fluid socially than Boston and Philadelphia, for example. In Philadelphia, it has been said, society is defined by who a person is; in Boston it is by what a person knows. But in New York, it is by how much he or she is worth. The currency of New York's fluidity is cash, and the society of cash is fashion. In fact, we talk about money much the same way we talk about fashion. By William Norwich.  AIDS-1981-TEXT-REVIEW (Undated) _ Twenty years ago, the disease now known as AIDS was a puzzle. The virus that causes it was already present in many victims, but the symptoms that define it were just beginning to emerge. The step-by-step arrival of awareness becomes clear in excerpted newspaper reports, bulletins and medical journals from that time.  BUSH-DAUGHTERS-REVIEW (Undated) _ A brief look at the media attention to the Bush twins' run-in with the law over alcohol. Reporters have been desperate for a White House scandal since Bill Clinton left office, and perhaps they also could not help but recall that a younger George W. Bush displayed a wild streak similar to his daughters' today. By Frank Bruni.  PEARL-HYPE-REVIEW (Undated) _ Interesting and amusing factoids concerning ``Pearl Harbor'' and the hoopla surrounding it. By Janet Maslin.  THISWEEK-REVIEW (Undated) _ Some highlights of the week.  _ Also moved in the `i' category:  CHINA-ECON-REVIEW (Shanghai) _ For the first time since China's private sector began to reappear in the late 1970s, private businesses have access to public capital. And with it, they should grow and eventually eclipse the state-owned enterprises and government-controlled collectives that still dominate the economy. As the private sector grows, it will become a powerful political constituency, and the interests of at least some businessmen will deviate from those of the Communist Party. Why then are China's leaders willing to unshackle private businesses? They recognize that a capital-backed private sector may be China's best hope for economic survival. By Craig S. Smith.  IRAN-POLITICS-REVIEW (Washington) _ Iran's presidential election is part of a continuing guerrilla war between conservatives who are determined to preserve a strict interpretation of Islamic rule and reformers who have coalesced around President Mohammed Khatami's calls for more political openness and greater personal freedom. Tthe battle has been so closely drawn over the last four years that the election itself is not expected to usher in dramatic new initiatives or climactic change; rather it will be read as an indicator of the reformers' strength or weakness as the fight continues. By Elaine Sciolino with Nazila Fathi.  STORY QUESTIONS OR RERUNS:  The Weekend Supervisor is Lynn Hoogenboom. On the Cover  POLAND-LOCKHEED (Undated) _ When Aleksander Kwasniewski, the president of Poland, visits with President Bush at the White House on Jan. 14, talk will inevitably turn to the F-16 fighter jet. Over the Christmas holiday, almost unnoticed, Poland announced it was accepting a $3.8 billion loan from Congress, the largest military loan in memory, to buy 48 of those Fighting Falcons. The scheduled lunch between the two leaders will cap a campaign by the Lockheed Martin Corp. that began six years ago and swung into high gear under Bush. By Leslie Wayne.  With graphic.  COMPANY-FOUNDERS-OPTIONS (Undated) _ It's one thing to dole out giant-sized stock options to executives hired to run a big company. But handing the same sweet deal seems particularly illogical and unnecessary to many watchdog groups when the corporation is still run by its founding family. After all, they argue, a founding chief executive is not likely to quit for a better-paid job elsewhere. And such executives typically own so much company stock that they already are in the same boat as public shareholders before a single stock option has been granted. By Diana B. Henriques.  With graphic.  AMERICA-WEST (Undated) _ America West, the first airline to get a loan guarantee after 9/11, is turning out to be the guinea pig for the industry in other ways, too _ it's trying out a new fare structure and charging for meals. It was the first to charge for paper tickets, and has become a big provocateur in the industry, one of the chief lobbyists against the proposed code-share alliance between Northwest-Continental-Delta _ accusing them of anti-competitive pricing. By Edward Wong.  With graphic.  Commentary  MARKET-WATCH (Undated) _ Financial commentary: To the architects who build them, integrated financial services empires have the allure of immense profits. But for the customers of these one-stop financial entities, perils often result. The laws separating commercial banks from investment firms have only recently been undone, so clients of financial services behemoths are just beginning to see how the inherent conflicts can affect them. By Gretchen Morgenson.  With graphic.  ECON-COLUMN (Undated) _ Economic View: The holiday shopping season was not all that bad. By Tom Redburn.  With graphic.  Features  AUTOS-WOMEN (Undated) _ Auto makers have made a big deal out of broadening their appeal to female buyers. But when it comes to selling cars, the trade remains overwhelmingly dominated by men. We look at why, and what the auto makers have done to change it. By Christina Le Beau. Autos  AUTO-SHOW (Detroit) _ Concept cars are the dream machines of auto shows, unattainable flashes of show-biz glamour among vehicles that come with easy-payment plans, extended warranties and Scotchguard fabric protection. Concept cars are not for sale, and they may say more about the state of mind of the industry than about the state of the art of engineering. At this year's Detroit show, which opens Jan. 11 after a week of previews and media events, gee-whiz future technologies are less in evidence than powerful but wistful visions of big American cars, like prewar limousines and the full-size sedan that until recently was almost written off as extinct. SCI-WIRED-ENVIRON (Undated) -- In the wilds of California's San Jacinto Mountains, along a steep canyon, scientists are turning 30 acres of pines and hardwoods into a futuristic vision of environmental study. They are linking up more than 100 tiny sensors, robots, cameras and computers that are beginning to paint an unusually detailed portrait of this lush world, home to more than 30 rare and endangered species. Scientists call it a new science -- real-time visualization over a large area. And it is spreading coast to coast, with more than $1 billion in networks of sensors planned not only for the land but places like the Hudson River and the deep Pacific. The rapid miniaturization of technologies behind cameras, cellphones and wireless computers is allowing the scientists to build innovative networks of small sensors that they say will produce a new era of ecological insight and, in time, will help save the planet.and, in time, will help save the planet.  With photo and graphic.  SCI-VANISHING-TWINS (Undated) -- Last month, when champion American cyclist Tyler Hamilton was accused of blood doping, or transfusing himself with another person's blood to increase his oxygen-carrying red cells, he offered a surprising defense: The small amount of extra blood found mixed in with his own must have come from a vanishing twin. In other words, his scientific expert argued, Hamilton had a twin that died in utero but, before dying, contributed some blood cells to him during fetal life. And those cells remained in his body, producing blood that matched the dead twin and not Hamilton. Researchers say they have no idea whether Hamilton is guilty of blood doping, but these notions of vanishing twins and chimerism now appear to be real, and common. By Gina Kolata.  With photo.  SCI-KIDS-PITCHING (Undated) -- Hey, parents -- throw that ball to your little one faster, not slower: a new study shows toddlers can't hit a change-up. By John Schwartz.  SCI-BOYD-ANTHROPOLOGY (Undated) -- A Conversation With feature on Robert Boyd, a theoretical biological anthropologist who uses mathematics and deduction to develop ideas about how Homo sapiens became earth's dominant species. He shares his views on the culture and evolution. Only humans can accumulate knowledge over long periods of time and transfer it so that the next generation can improve on it, he says. It's this trick that has allowed people to be as successful as we are. By Claudia Dreifus.  With photo.  SCI-Q&amp;A (Undated) -- A science question answered: When you see oil gushing from oil wells in old movies, why is the oil under such pressure? By C. Claiborne Ray.  SCI-GAZZANIGA-NEUROSCIENCE (Undated) -- A Scientist at Work profile on Michael Gazzaniga, director of cognitive neuroscience at Dartmouth. By Carl Zimmer.  With photo.  SCI-SALMONELLA-PETS (Undated) -- Last June, a 4-year-old boy in South Carolina was hospitalized for five days with a fever of 105, watery diarrhea and abdominal cramping. Doctors quickly found the cause: Salmonella typhimirium, a bacteria commonly found in undercooked chicken. But the culprit here was the boy's pet hamster -- the first document cases of a person infected with salmonella from a pet rodent. By Nicholas Bakalar.  SCI-WATCH (Undated) -- Science news and notes: Preening as well as eating seems to have shaped the beaks of the Galapagos finches; the far-reaching effects of shearing icebergs in Antarctica; Korea has a new salamander. By Henry Fountain.  With  SCI-MOON-ORIGINS (Undated) -- There are two kinds of moons in the solar system: those that formed when their parent planet did, and those that were made elsewhere and captured later. It now seems that Saturn's moon Phoebe is the made-elsewhere kind. A short by Henry Fountain.  SCI-PLATONIC-PUZZLE (Undated) -- The Greeks called them cosmic figures, five perfectly regular forms -- the tetrahedron, the cube, the octahedron, the dodecahedron and the icosahedron. Kepler once thought he could solve the mystery of the planets by finding a way to nest the solids. Now, a physicist has designed an interlocking wooden puzzle that is a complete set of Platonic solids. By Margaret Wertheim.  With a graphic.  SCI-PATIENT-RESPONSIBILITY (Undated) -- Current healthcare etiquette requires that a patient be viewed as a consumer, a knowledgeable care partner who is equipped and ready to take charge. The notion makes some sense; after all, who cares more about your health than you do? But in this global therapeutic village where the customer is king, a simple question remains unanswered: What happens if something goes awry, if a decision shared or even directed by a patient turns out to be the wrong one? On whose desk does that buck stop? By Kent Sepkowitz.  SCI-BEAST-INSIDE (Undated) -- The little old lady with the fine white hair and the sky-blue raincoat was there every day, sitting on a couch in the lobby of our apartment building. Then one day the beast appeared, the first sign of a degenerating mind. By Judith Groch.  SCI-METABOLIC-SYNDROME (Undated) -- As many as 55 million Americans meet the diagnostic criteria for metabolic syndrome, a cluster of risk factors that include mild hypertension, elevated glucose levels, high triglycerides and low levels of HDL cholesterol. But scientists still disagree about what causes the syndrome, how to diagnose it, how to treat patients who have it and even what it should be called. By Laurie Tarkan.  With photo.  SCI-REALLY (Undated) -- The claim: Oysters are aphrodisiacs. The facts: .... By Anahad O'Connor.  SCI-VITAL-SIGNS (Undated) -- News and notes on health and medicine:  -- Screening: Public services advertisements by celebrities urging people to get various cancer screening tests may be admirable, but are they desirable? Maybe not, researchers argue in a new study. They report on a survey finding that the ads are widely seen, and that many people say they are more likely to be tested as a result. The problem, the researchers said, is that many of those people should not be tested, which can lead to unneeded treatment. It would be more useful, they argue, for celebrities to make ads urging people not to do dangerous things like drinking and driving.  -- Reactions: Highly unsaturated vegetable oils, generally regarded as healthy for people who want to avoid heart disease, produce a toxic compound when heated to frying temperatures for half an hour or more. The compound, HNE, has been associated with several illnesses, including cardiovascular disease and Alzheimer's. The findings highlight the risk of repeatedly reusing these oils, the researchers say.  -- Behavior: Men who associate alcohol with sexual activity become sexually stimulated if they are subliminally shown words related to alcohol use, a new study suggests. Researchers found that when words like keg were flashed on a computer screen without the men's knowledge, they were more likely to say that women shown in a series of photographs were attractive.  -- Patterns: Regular use of acetaminophen, better known as Tylenol, appears to be linked with an increased rate of asthma and other lung problems.  SCI-BRODY-HEALTH (Undated) -- The Personal Health column: With the surgeon general urging Americans to do at least 30 minutes a day of moderately vigorous exercise to improve their health, and with some 60 percent of adults doing little or none, a program attractive enough to prompt millions of otherwise sedentary women to get out and just do it certainly seems worthy of praise. Considering the rapid growth of Curves, a privately owned no-frills fitness company that has more than 8,000 franchises here and abroad, the concept is a winner. But how well does it work? By Jane E. Brody.  SCI-COCAINE-RISK (Undated) -- Cocaine use quadruples the risk of aneurysm, a new study shows. By Lawrence K. Altman.  With photo. Lynndie England's guilty plea in a military courtroom in Texas last week was her equally bizarre and unraveled relationship with Spc. Charles Graner, who just weeks ago married another defendant from the abuses at Abu Ghraib prison. The reservists' entanglements with one other and their treatment of Iraqi prisoners have produced a dark soap opera. By Kate Zernike.  DIVING-FOR-ABALONE (Pointe Arena, Calif.) -- Call it extreme dining. It is abalone season, and divers flock to the Mendocino coast, one of the last bastions of this prized delicacy, which is legally off-limits to commercial fishermen. To protect the abalone's deep-water habitat, no breathing apparatus is allowed, and strict limits are enforced, three abalone a day. But divers, often 50-something men with desk jobs, encounter very dangerous conditions. Because of tidal surges, murky waters and forests of kelp, nearly every abalone season brings with it reports of drownings. Last year, a renowned surgeon lost his life while diving for abalone. By Patricia Leigh Brown.  WTC-MEMORIAL (New York) -- As questions fly about the future of the Freedom Tower and commercial buildings at ground zero, quieter battles are raging over the future of the memorial to the lives lost there. The memorial's architect, Michael Arad, is at odds with the memorial's landscape architect, Peter Walker. The families of the dead are at odds with Arad. What is this thing going to look like, in the end? By Robin Pogrebin.  FINANCIAL  (Coded f)  COMPUTER-THEFT (San Francisco) -- The incident seemed alarming enough: a breach of a Cisco Systems network in which an intruder seized programming instructions for many of the computers that control the flow of the Internet. Now federal officials and computer security investigators have acknowledged that the Cisco break-in last year was only part of a more extensive operation -- involving a single intruder or a small band, apparently based in Europe -- in which thousands of computer systems were penetrated. Investigators in the United States and Europe say they spent almost a year pursuing the case as the attacks continued, breaching computer systems serving the military, NASA and prominent research laboratories. The case illustrates not only the ease with which sensitive Internet-connected computers can be penetrated but also the difficulty in tracing those responsible. And at a time when millions of consumers are discovering that their personal information has been stolen from commercial databases, the case shows that the guardians of the most sophisticated corporate and government networks are vulnerable as well. By John Markoff and Lowell Bergman.  With photo.  CENTAM-TRADE (Washington) -- Social Security is not the administration's only economic initiative that is in trouble in Congress. The current centerpiece of President Bush's trade agenda, the Central American Free Trade Agreement, is facing unusually united Democratic opposition as well as serious problems in overcoming well-entrenched special interest groups like sugar producers and much of the textile industry. With record trade deficits, concerns about lost jobs and an overarching fear that the United States is losing out in the accelerated pace of global changes, the sentiment in Congress is shifting away from approving new free trade agreements. By Elizabeth Becker.  (This story was sent and held on Friday, May 6, with the slug CONGRESS-TRADE)  ELITE-FLYERS (Undated) -- Even as hot-shot corporate executives join the chorus of protest at their devalued miles, members of a super-elite platinum loyalty program are smirking with satisfaction as they are chauffeured to the airport and met at the other end by their personal concierges. By Stuart Elliott.  BASEBALL-AD-COLUMN (Undated) -- Five weeks into the major league baseball season, attendance is up and fans are chanting MVP for their favorite players. But parts of Madison Avenue have picked up a different mantra, one that plays off the steroid debate. By Nat Ives.  BOX-TV-SPORTS (Undated) -- An On TV Sports column contrasting the real thing -- the great lightweight title fight on Saturday night between Corrales and Castillo -- and the contrived stuff offered on The Contender. By Richard Sandomir.  WTC-MEMORIAL (New York ) -- As questions fly about the future of the Freedom Tower and commercial buildings at ground zero, quieter battles are raging over the future of the memorial to the lives lost there. The memorial's architect, Michael Arad, is at odds with the memorial's landscape architect, Peter Walker. The families of the dead are at odds with Arad. What is this thing going to look like, in the end? By Robin Pogrebin.  MOVIES-BOX-OFFICE (Los Angeles) -- Now Hollywood is starting to get worried. The poor box-office performance last weekend of the first major film of the summer, Kingdom of Heaven, released by 20th Century Fox, made for 11 weeks in a row of declining movie attendance and revenue compared with last year, adding up to the longest slump since 2000 and raising an uncomfortable question: Are people turning away from lackluster movies, or turning their backs on the whole business of going to theaters? By Sharon Waxman.  THEM-BOOK-REVIEW (Undated) -- Them: A Memoir of Parents, by Francine du Plessix Gray (Penguin). Reviewed by Michiko Kakutani.  MILLER-MEMORIAL (New York) -- A memorial service for the playwright Arthur Miller at a Broadway theater. Miller died on Feb. 10 at the age of 89. By Jason Zinoman.  JFK-INAUGURAL (Undated) -- A new book documents that Kennedy didn't write his inaugural address; Sorenson, Galbraith and Adlai Stevenson all had a hand in it. That is exactly the opposite conclusion reached by a similar book published last year. How two authors looked at the same evidence and saw opposing truths, and why it matters 40 years later. By Edward Wyatt.  PINAULT-MUSEUM (Paris) -- Francois Pinault, a billionaire who is France's wealthiest art lover, announced Monday that he was abandoning plans to build a $195 million contemporary art museum on the outskirts of Paris and would instead present part of his vast collection in the Palazzo Grassi, an elegant exhibition space on Venice's Grand Canal that he recently acquired. Pinault, whose corporate empire includes Christie's, Gucci, Yves Saint Laurent and three chains of department and media stores, said he was reluctantly giving up the museum project because it was already five years behind schedule because of delays caused by municipal authorities in the Paris suburb of Boulogne-Billancourt. By Alan Riding.  CRASH-FILM (Los Angeles) -- Los Angeles is in love with the idea of its own self-destruction; it's hard to think of another American city so similarly possessed. It is a curious form of romantic self-aggrandizement, one familiar from untold numbers of novels, short stories, films and songs. Now it has spread to video games, including the forthcoming 187 Ride or Die, about a street racing thug, and the film in Crash, which opened on Friday, the latest in apocalypse entertainment about Los Angeles. A Critic's Notebook by Manohla Dargis.  TV-ASYLUM-REVIEW (Undated) -- An enormously disturbing Frontline report profiles the enormously disturbed. This documentary, The New Asylums, which will be presented on Tuesday night, explains that the mentally ill, in the decades after a mass release from mental hospitals, have often wound up in less forgiving confines. Reviewed by Ned Martel.  DVD-RELEASES (Undated) -- The weekly DVD column. By Dave Kehr.  ARTS-BRIEFLY (Undated) -- News and notes on culture and entertainment.  SCIENCE  (Coded a)  SMELL-SEX-PREFERENCE (Undated) -- Using a brain imaging technique, Swedish researchers have shown that men and women respond differently to two odors that may be involved in sexual arousal, and that homosexual men respond in the same way as women. The new research may open the way to studying the chemistry of human sexual attraction. By Nicholas Wade.  SCI-WIRED-ENVIRON (Undated) -- In the wilds of California's San Jacinto Mountains, along a steep canyon, scientists are turning 30 acres of pines and hardwoods into a futuristic vision of environmental study. They are linking up more than 100 tiny sensors, robots, cameras and computers that are beginning to paint an unusually detailed portrait of this lush world, home to more than 30 rare and endangered species. Scientists call it a new science -- real-time visualization over a large area. And it is spreading coast to coast, with more than $1 billion in networks of sensors planned not only for the land but places like the Hudson River and the deep Pacific. The rapid miniaturization of technologies behind cameras, cellphones and wireless computers is allowing the scientists to build innovative networks of small sensors that they say will produce a new era of ecological insight and, in time, will help save the planet. By William J. Broad.  With photo and graphic.  SCI-VANISHING-TWINS (Undated) -- Last month, when champion American cyclist Tyler Hamilton was accused of blood doping, or transfusing himself with another person's blood to increase his oxygen-carrying red cells, he offered a surprising defense: The small amount of extra blood found mixed in with his own must have come from a vanishing twin. In other words, his scientific expert argued, Hamilton had a twin that died in utero but, before dying, contributed some blood cells to him during fetal life. And those cells remained in his body, producing blood that matched the dead twin and not Hamilton. Researchers say they have no idea whether Hamilton is guilty of blood doping, but these notions of vanishing twins and chimerism now appear to be real, and common. By Gina Kolata.  With photo.  SCI-KIDS-PITCHING (Undated) -- Hey, parents -- throw that ball to your little one faster, not slower: a new study shows toddlers can't hit a change-up. By John Schwartz.  SCI-BOYD-ANTHROPOLOGY (Undated) -- A Conversation With feature on Robert Boyd, a theoretical biological anthropologist who uses mathematics and deduction to develop ideas about how Homo sapiens became earth's dominant species. He shares his views on the culture and evolution. Only humans can accumulate knowledge over long periods of time and transfer it so that the next generation can improve on it, he says. It's this trick that has allowed people to be as successful as we are. By Claudia Dreifus.  With photo.  SCI-Q&amp;A (Undated) -- A science question answered: When you see oil gushing from oil wells in old movies, why is the oil under such pressure? By C. Claiborne Ray.  SCI-GAZZANIGA-NEUROSCIENCE (Undated) -- A Scientist at Work profile on Michael Gazzaniga, director of cognitive neuroscience at Dartmouth. By Carl Zimmer.  With photo.  SCI-SALMONELLA-PETS (Undated) -- Last June, a 4-year-old boy in South Carolina was hospitalized for five days with a fever of 105, watery diarrhea and abdominal cramping. Doctors quickly found the cause: Salmonella typhimirium, a bacteria commonly found in undercooked chicken. But the culprit here was the boy's pet hamster -- the first document cases of a person infected with salmonella from a pet rodent. By Nicholas Bakalar.  SCI-WATCH (Undated) -- Science news and notes: Preening as well as eating seems to have shaped the beaks of the Galapagos finches; the far-reaching effects of shearing icebergs in Antarctica; Korea has a new salamander.
