If you have questions about stories, call 888-346-9867 or 212-556-1927. For questions about photos, call 888-603-1036 or 212-556-4204. Sunday Financial articles are budgeted separately on Thursday, then incorporated, with updates, into Friday's final budget.  Arts &amp; Leisure articles move with an e code; Travel articles, t.  ARTS &amp; LEISURE  VIDEO-GAMES-PEACEMAKER (Undated) -- Video games have long entertained users by immersing them in fantasy worlds full of dragons or spaceships. But Peacemaker is part of a new generation: Games that immerse people in the real world, full of real-time political crises. And the games' designers aren't just selling a voyeuristic thrill. Games, they argue, can be more than just mindless fun: They can be a medium for change. By Clive Thompson.  COLLAPSING-CASBAH (Algiers) -- It has stood for centuries, a slope of gleaming white houses climbing in steps from the sea like a construction of sugar cubes. It gave this Mediterranean port the nickname la Blanche, the white one. But despite the romance surrounding the old quarter, known as the Casbah and once home to pirates and freedom fighters, it is literally imploding from neglect. By Craig S. Smith.  THEATER-CARRADINE (Undated) -- Keith Carradine, who is going into Dirty Rotten Scoundrels, speaks about his own life as a reformed scoundrel. By Joyce Wadler.  FILM-REILLY (Undated) -- Audiences have watched John C. Reilly in dozens of films. One of these days they may remember his name. By Jacques Steinberg.  FILM-BRAZIL-SAND (Undated) -- Larry Rohter previews the beautiful Brazilian film House of Sand, about three generations of women trapped in the remote Brazilian desert.  TV-UGLY-BETTY (Undated) -- Matthew Fogel tells how a modest Colombian telenovela about a pitifully ugly woman trying to navigate the shallow waters of the fashion industry became one of the world's most popular television shows. By Matthew Fogel.  COMICS-PRODIGY (Shutesbury, Mass.) -- Alexa Kitchen, who turned 9 last month, has been a sensation in the comic book world since she was 6, when her parents first showed her work at the art festival that the Museum of Comic and Cartoon Art in New York puts on every spring. Her draftsmanship is a little primitive, but her drawings powerfully express some of what it feels like to be a kid, from the raging war between boys and girls to the bummer of being too young to baby-sit. By Lauren Mechling  Bestseller Lists  BESTSELLERS-HARD-L (Undated) -- Best-selling hardbacks, long-form list.  BESTSELLERS-HARD-S (Undated) -- Best-selling hardbacks, short-form list.  BESTSELLERS-PAPER-L (Undated) -- Best-selling paperbacks, long-form list.  BESTSELLERS-PAPER-S (Undated) -- Best-selling paperbacks, short-form list.  NOTEWORTHY-PAPERBACKS (Undated) -- Short summaries of other paperbacks.  TRAVEL-SPAIN-PARADORS (Undated) -- Many of Spain's historic paradors are getting makeovers to appeal to travelers who want spas and Wi-Fi access along with palatial surroundings and 16th-century friezes. By Sarah Wildman.  With:  TRAVEL-PARADORS-TOUR (Undated) -- A five-parador tour through Northern Spain. By Fred A. Bernstein.  TRAVEL-ITALY-GENOA (Undated) -- In most of Italy's villages and towns, restaurants serve the same dishes they've been serving for a hundred years or more. These vary from one town to the next, even if the distances traveled are tiny, and the locals usually insist that the food in the last town you visited is inferior to what you're about to taste. About half the time they're right. In the cities, though, you find more regional food, and even food from other parts of Italy, giving you a taste of more varied cuisines. Genoa, in the heart of Liguria, is one of the best cities for this sort of pan-Italian eating, at least in part because the region is among Europe's culinary highlights; it remains difficult to eat any way but well here. By Mark Bittmann.  TRAVEL-FRANCE-VERSAILLES (Undated) -- With its Hall of Mirrors, formal gardens and imposing works of art, the chateau of Versailles is a manly sort of place, the ultimate symbol of kingly power in France. Not far away is what might be called the anti-Versailles, a smaller, messier, more feminine site where Marie Antoinette, the last queen of France's Ancien Regime, sought refuge. The 86-acre Domaine of Marie Antoinette lacks the visibility and aura of the Versailles chateau. The estate attracts only about 300,000 visitors a year; Versailles draws more than 4 million. But the director of Versailles, Christine Albanel, is determined to redress that imbalance. By Elaine Sciolino.  TRAVEL-EUROPE-HARBORS (Undated) -- There was a time when a harbor was the heart of its city, bustling with fish markets and sailors and ships laden with hopeful immigrants, world travelers and exotic cargo. But with the advent of supersized cargo vessels and direct container shipping about 50 years ago, many once-busy ports began to languish, often becoming seedy urban eyesores. In Europe, though, many old harbors are being reinvented as architecturally experimental areas with modern residential buildings, hotels, art spaces and artificial beaches. By Gisela Williams.  Regular Features  PRACTICAL-TRAVELER (Undated) -- Rental car insurance: Paying for coverage you may not need. By Matt Villano.  TRAVEL-BRIEFS (Undated) -- News and notes on travel. By Michelle Higgins.  TRAVEL-Q&amp;A (Undated) -- Travel questions answered. By Roger Collis. Its position regarding the demand that it suspend enrichment remains a determined no. On Saturday, President Mahmoud Ahmadinejad made a provocative, if symbolic, gesture by formally inaugurating a heavy-water plant. By Michael Slackman.  IRAQ (Baghdad, Iraq) -- Developments. By Damien Cave.  IRAQ-TROOPS-ENTERTAIN (Washington) -- A look at the military's efforts to bring entertainment to the troops in the form of a Vegas-type dance review. By Michael R. Gordon.  CHECHNYA-ABDUCTION (Grozny, Russia) -- The hurried effort to free Elina Ersenoyeva, who was abducted by masked men on the main street of Chechnya's capital on Aug. 17, began like many others. An account of her disappearance was compiled and circulated. Human rights groups and civil society organizations urged Russia to investigate, find her captors and work to secure her release. But the story has taken a surprising turn, even by the bizarre standards of violence, organized crime and brutal police activity that have accompanied Chechnya's lingering war. Ersenoveva, it turns out, was a secret bride of Shamil Basayev, the notorious Chechen terrorist leader who died in an explosion on July 11.  ITALY-FOREIGN-MINISTER (Rome) -- Interview with the Italian foreign minister, Massimo D'Alema. By Ian Fisher.  BOSNIA-POLITICS (Sarajevo, Bosnia-Herzegovina) -- A series of incidents that have raised ethnic tensions have been accompanied by a sharp increase in nationalist rhetoric that is worrying international officials here as the country prepares for parliamentary and presidential election on Oct. 1. By Nicholas Wood.  TURKEY-CALVINIST-ISLAM (Kayseri, Turkey) -- Many Europeans and secular Turks have dismissed this Anatolia as the other Turkey, a non-European backwater where women in head scarves are more prevalent than businessmen in pinstripes. Islam, they argue, never went through its own Reformation and so is not receptive to capitalism and innovation. Yet Kayseri and surrounding towns like Hacilar have produced so many successful Muslim entrepreneurs that the area has earned the title of Anatolian tiger. The region's mix of Muslim values, hard work and raging capitalism has even prompted sociologists to coin a new term to describe the phenomenon: Calvinist Islam. By Dan Bilefsky.  VENEZUELA-U.S. (Caracas, Venezuela) -- Washington formally protested Friday against Venezuela's seizure of cargo from a U.S. Embassy convoy. Venezuela defended the action, saying they had halted smuggling that flagrantly violated diplomatic protocol. (Summary from wires.) By Simon Romero  KATRINA-BODIES (New Orleans) -- Hurricane Katrina scattered bodies over hundreds of square miles, where water, heat and time distorted what many of the dead looked like in life. The system hastily conceived to collect, identify and release for burial hundreds of bodies was ill equipped to deal with wholesale catastrophe. Many families waited far too long for the release of identified bodies, delaying burial, prolonging grief. Working like wartime MASH units, pathologists, investigators and counselors eventually attached names to nearly 900 of the bodies they examined. But some 50 victims remain unknown to the world still, a year later. By Dan Barry.  The Katrina Year, the fifth article in a five-day series on the lasting effects of Hurricane Katrina a year after it hit New Orleans and the Gulf Coast.  With photos and graphic.  KATRINA-ORLEANS-FUTURE (New Orleans) -- At one edge of this city's future are the extravagant visions of its boosters. Awash in federal cash, the New Orleans they dream of will be an arts-infused mecca for youthful risk-takers, a boomtown where entrepreneurs can repair to cool French Quarter bars in ancient buildings after a hard day of deal making. At the other extreme are the gloomy predictions of the pessimists. New Orleans will be Detroit, they say, a sickly urban wasteland abandoned by the middle class. A moldering core will be surrounded by miles of vacant houses, with wide-open neighborhoods roamed by drug dealers and other criminals. The new New Orleans will be merely a grim amplification of its present unpromising self, the pessimists say. Somewhere between these unrealistic visions lies a glimpse of the city's real future a year after Hurricane Katrina, say many planners, demographers and others here who have been deeply involved in rebuilding. Like a half-completed drawing in a child's coloring book, the picture is starting to fill in. There are shadows and firmer outlines, a few promising, some of them menacing. By Adam Nossiter.  The Katrina Year, the sixth and final article in a five-day series on the lasting effects of Hurricane Katrina a year after it hit New Orleans and the Gulf Coast.  SUSPICIOUS-TRADING (Undated) -- The boom in corporate mergers is creating concern that illicit trading ahead of deal announcements is becoming a systemic problem. It is against the law to trade on inside information about an imminent merger, of course. But an analysis of the nation's biggest mergers over the last 12 months indicates that the securities of 41 percent of the companies receiving buyout bids exhibited abnormal and suspicious trading in the days and weeks before those deals became public. For those who bought shares during these periods of unusual trading, quick gains of as much as 40 percent were possible. By Gretchen Morgenson.  SCHOOLS-ENROLLMENT (Sterling, Va.) -- Some 55 million youngsters are enrolling for classes in the nation's schools this fall, making this the largest group of students in America's history and, in ethnic terms, the most dazzlingly diverse since waves of European immigrants washed through the public schools a century ago. By Sam Dillon.  TEAMSTERS-ELECTION (Washington) -- Tom Leedham, head of a Teamsters local in Oregon, is taking on the biggest name in labor: Hoffa. Leedham is seeking to unseat James P. Hoffa, the president of the International Brotherhood of Teamsters and the son of the nation's most famous -- some would say infamous -- union leader, Jimmy Hoffa, who disappeared in 1975 outside a restaurant in Detroit. By Steven Greenhouse.  FLA-ROBBERIES (West Palm Beach, Fla.) -- Robberies of illegal immigrants have become a serious problem here in recent years, and the victims, mostly from Guatemala, often avoid reporting the crimes, fearing deportation. By Riki Altman and Terry Aguayo.  WEST-WILDFIRES (Denver) -- The wildfires of 2006 have been just about everywhere, from the plains of Texas and Nebraska to the high northern Rockies of Montana to the Nevada desert. The damage has been incremental and dispersed, but cumulatively no less devastating, with at least 7.1 million acres burned as of late this week, more than in any comparable period in 10 years, federal figures indicate. By Kirk Johnson.  CALIF-POLLUTION-FEE (Lathrop, Calif.) -- Developers and air quality regulators are locked in a legal battle over new construction fees for the California Central Valley intended to reduce the region's chronic smog problem. The fees, which went into effect in March in eight counties in this fast-growing valley, are the most far-reaching in the country in their effort to link development and air pollution. A legal campaign to have them thrown out is being watched closely in other parts of the state, including Southern California and the San Francisco Bay area, where officials say they hope to impose similar fees. By Carolyn Marshall.  RICH-COLUMN (Undated) -- The president brings the love to New Orleans. By Frank Rich.  BROOKS-COLUMN (Washington) -- In Tattoo Nation, everyone's a rebel. By David Brooks.  PUBLIC-EDITOR-COLUMN (Undated) -- Precisely false vs. approximately right. By Jack Rosenthal. "174438"	"they"	"argue"	" But it is in our attempts to come to grips with prejudice that authenticity most confounds.
